239 ready: function () {

240 if (this.registered.length === this.scripts.length) {

241 this.registered.forEach(function (fn) {

242 if (typeof fn === 'function') {

243 setTimeout(fn, 0);

244 }

245 });

246 this.ready = function () {};

247 }

248 },

249

250 insert: function (s) {

251 var t = d.getElementById('js-position' + this.index);

252 if (t && t.insertAdjacentElement) {

253 t.insertAdjacentElement('afterend', s);

254 } else {

255 d.head.appendChild(s);

256 }

257 ++this.index;

258 },

259

260 createScript: function (script, beforeLoad) {

261 var s = d.createElement('script');

262 s.id = 'js-position' + (this.index + 1);

263 s.setAttribute('data-test', script.test);

264 if (beforeLoad) {

265 s.defer = 'defer';

266 s.onload = function () {

267 if (script.noinit) {

268 loader.register(true);

269 }

270 if (d.readyState === 'interactive' || d.readyState === 'complete') {

271 loader.ready();

272 }

273 };

274 } else {

275 s.async = 'async';

276 }

277 s.src = script.src;

278 return s;

279 },

280

281 init: function () {

282 this.scripts.forEach(function (s) {

283 loader.insert(loader.createScript(s, true));

284 });

285

286 d.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {

287 loader.ready();

288

289 var conditionalScripts = [

290 {match: 'div[data-pan-container]', src: '/static/js/pan-zoom-es6-bundle-464a2af269.js', test: 'pan-zoom-js', module: true },

291 {match: 'div[data-pan-container]', src: '/static/js/pan-zoom-es5-bundle-2626f1bdf8.js', test: 'pan-zoom-js', nomodule: true },

292 {match: 'math,span.mathjax-tex', src: '/static/js/math-es6-bundle-d616f8e4f9.js', test: 'math-js', module: true},

293 {match: 'math,span.mathjax-tex', src: '/static/js/math-es5-bundle-66109c5184.js', test: 'math-js', nomodule: true}

294 ];

295

296

297 if (conditionalScripts) {

298 conditionalScripts.filter(function (script) {

299 return !!document.querySelector(script.match) && !((moduleSupport && script.nomodule) || (!moduleSupport && script.module));

300 }).forEach(function (script) {

301 loader.insert(loader.createScript(script));

302 });

303 }

304 }, false);

305 }

306 };

307 loader.init();

308 }

309 })(window, document);

310</script>

356 <meta name="dc.description" content="Metastases can originate from disseminated tumour cells (DTCs), which may be dormant for years before reactivation. Here we find that the orphan nuclear receptor NR2F1 is epigenetically upregulated in experimental head and neck squamous cell carcinoma (HNSCC) dormancy models and in DTCs from prostate cancer patients carrying dormant disease for 7&#8211;18 years. NR2F1-dependent dormancy is recapitulated by a co-treatment with the DNA-demethylating agent 5-Aza-C and retinoic acid across various cancer types. NR2F1-induced quiescence is dependent on SOX9, RAR&#946; and CDK inhibitors. When NR2F1 is blocked in vivo, growth arrest or survival of dormant DTCs is interrupted in different organs. We conclude that NR2F1 is a critical node in dormancy induction and maintenance by integrating epigenetic programmes of quiescence and survival in DTCs. Tumour cells disseminated from the primary tumour can remain dormant for years before initiating metastases. Here Sosa et al.show that the orphan nuclear receptor NR2F1 can be induced by bone marrow cues and by epigenetic drugs to promote quiescence and tumour cell dormancy in several cancer types."/>

826 <div class="c-article-body">

827 <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Metastases can originate from disseminated tumour cells (DTCs), which may be dormant for years before reactivation. Here we find that the orphan nuclear receptor NR2F1 is epigenetically upregulated in experimental head and neck squamous cell carcinoma (HNSCC) dormancy models and in DTCs from prostate cancer patients carrying dormant disease for 7–18 years. NR2F1-dependent dormancy is recapitulated by a co-treatment with the DNA-demethylating agent 5-Aza-C and retinoic acid across various cancer types. NR2F1-induced quiescence is dependent on SOX9, RARβ and CDK inhibitors. Intriguingly, NR2F1 induces global chromatin repression and the pluripotency gene <i>NANOG,</i> which contributes to dormancy of DTCs in the bone marrow. When NR2F1 is blocked <i>in vivo</i>, growth arrest or survival of dormant DTCs is interrupted in different organs. We conclude that NR2F1 is a critical node in dormancy induction and maintenance by integrating epigenetic programmes of quiescence and survival in DTCs.</p></div></div></section>

880 <div class="main-content">

881 <section data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>A large proportion of patients diagnosed with cancer harbour nonproliferative residual tumour cells, which can fuel incurable local or distant recurrences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Sosa, M. S., Avivar-Valderas, A., Bragado, P., Wen, H. C. &amp; Aguirre-Ghiso, J. A. ERK1/2 and p38alpha/beta signaling in tumor cell quiescence: opportunities to control dormant residual disease. Clin. Cancer Res. 17, 5850–5857 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e660">1</a></sup>. The ability of dormant residual tumour cells to persist but also interconvert between dormancy and proliferation may be the result of epigenetic reprogramming mechanisms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Sosa, M. S., Bragado, P. &amp; Aguirre-Ghiso, J. A. Mechanisms of disseminated cancer cell dormancy: an awakening field. Nat. Rev. Cancer 14, 611–622 (2014)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e664">2</a></sup>. However, mechanisms and markers that identify dormant cells at risk of resuming growth are unavailable. We reported that p38α/β regulates a quiescence transcription factor (TF) network that contains proliferation/growth arrest and pluripotency/self-renewal genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Adam, A. P. et al. Computational identification of a p38SAPK-regulated transcription factor network required for tumor cell quiescence. Cancer Res. 69, 5664–5672 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e668">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Onder, T. T. et al. Chromatin-modifying enzymes as modulators of reprogramming. Nature 483, 598–602 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e671">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Marion, R. M. et al. A p53-mediated DNA damage response limits reprogramming to ensure iPS cell genomic integrity. Nature 460, 1149–1153 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e674">5</a></sup>. The p38α/β pathway and quiescence were activated by TGFβ2 signalling specifically in disseminated tumour cells (DTCs) that are dormant in the bone marrow<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bragado, P. et al. TGF-beta2 dictates disseminated tumour cell fate in target organs through TGF-beta-RIII and p38alpha/beta signalling. Nat. Cell Biol. 15, 1351–1361 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e678">6</a></sup>. Further, the p38α/β-regulated TF network served as a ‘dormancy signature’ that was enriched in oestrogen receptor (ER)-positive luminal over ER-negative basal-type primary breast tumours<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Kim, R. S. et al. Dormancy signatures and metastasis in estrogen receptor positive and negative breast cancer. PLoS ONE 7, e35569 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e682">7</a></sup> and predicted for longer metastasis-free periods in the ER+ group<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Kim, R. S. et al. Dormancy signatures and metastasis in estrogen receptor positive and negative breast cancer. PLoS ONE 7, e35569 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e687">7</a></sup>. Thus, we hypothesized that genes within this signature may influence the timing of metastasis via regulation of dormancy.</p><p>Among the genes in the dormancy signature<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Adam, A. P. et al. Computational identification of a p38SAPK-regulated transcription factor network required for tumor cell quiescence. Cancer Res. 69, 5664–5672 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e694">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bragado, P. et al. TGF-beta2 dictates disseminated tumour cell fate in target organs through TGF-beta-RIII and p38alpha/beta signalling. Nat. Cell Biol. 15, 1351–1361 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e697">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Kim, R. S. et al. Dormancy signatures and metastasis in estrogen receptor positive and negative breast cancer. PLoS ONE 7, e35569 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e700">7</a></sup> we identified <i>NR2F1</i>, an orphan nuclear receptor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Qiu, Y. et al. Spatiotemporal expression patterns of chicken ovalbumin upstream promoter-transcription factors in the developing mouse central nervous system: evidence for a role in segmental patterning of the diencephalon. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 91, 4451–4455 (1994)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e707">8</a></sup> that induces cell lineage determination in response to signals like retinoic acid (RA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Zhuang, Y. &amp; Gudas, L. J. Overexpression of COUP-TF1 in murine embryonic stem cells reduces retinoic acid-associated growth arrest and increases extraembryonic endoderm gene expression. Differentiation 76, 760–771 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e711">9</a></sup>, limits induced pluripotent stem cells (iPS) reprogramming<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Onder, T. T. et al. Chromatin-modifying enzymes as modulators of reprogramming. Nature 483, 598–602 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e715">4</a></sup> and regulates enhancer elements during human neural crest cell differentiation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Rada-Iglesias, A. et al. Epigenomic annotation of enhancers predicts transcriptional regulators of human neural crest. Cell Stem Cell 11, 633–648 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e720">10</a></sup>. Because these processes regulate pluripotency and limit proliferation, we investigated whether NR2F1 and these processes were linked to the interconversion between cancer cell dormancy and proliferation.</p><p>Like other genes in the RA pathway (for example, RARβ)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Fackler, M. J. et al. Quantitative multiplex methylation-specific PCR assay for the detection of promoter hypermethylation in multiple genes in breast cancer. Cancer Res. 64, 4442–4452 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e727">11</a></sup> <i>NR2F1</i> mRNA is downregulated in several cancers including HNSCC, prostate, lung and breast versus normal tissues (Oncomine database)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Ginos, M. A. et al. Identification of a gene expression signature associated with recurrent disease in squamous cell carcinoma of the head and neck. Cancer Res. 64, 55–63 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e734">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Richardson, A. L. et al. X chromosomal abnormalities in basal-like human breast cancer. Cancer Cell 9, 121–132 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e737">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Hou, J. et al. Expression profiling-based subtyping identifies novel non-small cell lung cancer subgroups and implicates putative resistance to pemetrexed therapy. J. Thorac. Oncol. 7, 105–114." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e740">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Grasso, C. S. et al. The mutational landscape of lethal castration-resistant prostate cancer. Nature 487, 239–243 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e743">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Taylor, B. S. et al. Integrative genomic profiling of human prostate cancer. Cancer Cell 18, 11–22 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e746">16</a></sup> and it is functionally linked to a breast cancer susceptibility locus (Mcs1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Smits, B. M. et al. The gene desert mammary carcinoma susceptibility locus Mcs1a regulates Nr2f1 modifying mammary epithelial cell differentiation and proliferation. PLoS Genet. 9, e1003549 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e750">17</a></sup>. Further, upregulation of <i>NR2F1</i> correlated with longer disease-free periods after hormonal ablation in prostate cancer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Thompson, V. C. et al. A gene signature identified using a mouse model of androgen receptor-dependent prostate cancer predicts biochemical relapse in human disease. Int. J. Cancer 131, 662–672 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e758">18</a></sup>. Thus, changes in NR2F1 levels in primary tumours may influence residual tumour cell fate.</p><p>Here we provide evidence that NR2F1 coordinates gene expression found in quiescent cells and also in self-renewing ES cells<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Kim, J. et al. A Myc network accounts for similarities between embryonic stem and cancer cell transcription programs. Cell 143, 313–324 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e765">19</a></sup>. We show that NR2F1 regulates the behaviour of residual tumour cells in postoperative mice as its inactivation causes a rapid switch from dormancy to proliferation of occult tumour cells and systemic recurrence. This is true except in the bone marrow, where NR2F1 appears to regulate DTC survival. Importantly, restoration of NR2F1 expression using DNA-demethylating agents and activation of RA signalling is sufficient to recapitulate the quiescence programme and induce chromatin changes linked to a durable dormant state. These findings break new ground in our understanding of the dormancy mechanisms and identify markers that might pinpoint residual cancer with the ability to escape dormancy.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">NR2F1<sup>high</sup> human tumour cells are dormant</h3><p>We first used the squamous cell carcinoma cell line HEp3 model of proliferation versus dormancy to dissect the molecular mechanisms of conversion of malignant cells into a dormancy-like behaviour characterized by tumour cell quiescence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Adam, A. P. et al. Computational identification of a p38SAPK-regulated transcription factor network required for tumor cell quiescence. Cancer Res. 69, 5664–5672 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e784">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bragado, P. et al. TGF-beta2 dictates disseminated tumour cell fate in target organs through TGF-beta-RIII and p38alpha/beta signalling. Nat. Cell Biol. 15, 1351–1361 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e787">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Aguirre-Ghiso, J. A., Ossowski, L. &amp; Rosenbaum, S. K. Green fluorescent protein tagging of extracellular signal-regulated kinase and p38 pathways reveals novel dynamics of pathway activation during primary and metastatic growth. Cancer Res. 64, 7336–7345 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e790">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Aguirre-Ghiso, J. A., Estrada, Y., Liu, D. &amp; Ossowski, L. ERK(MAPK) activity as a determinant of tumor growth and dormancy; regulation by p38(SAPK). Cancer Res. 63, 1684–1695 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e793">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Aguirre-Ghiso, J. A., Liu, D., Mignatti, A., Kovalski, K. &amp; Ossowski, L. Urokinase receptor and fibronectin regulate the ERK(MAPK) to p38(MAPK) activity ratios that determine carcinoma cell proliferation or dormancy in vivo. Mol. Biol. Cell 12, 863–879 (2001)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e796">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Schewe, D. M. &amp; Aguirre-Ghiso, J. A. ATF6alpha-Rheb-mTOR signaling promotes survival of dormant tumor cells in vivo. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 105, 10519–10524 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e799">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Liu, D., Aguirre Ghiso, J., Estrada, Y. &amp; Ossowski, L. EGFR is a transducer of the urokinase receptor initiated signal that is required for in vivo growth of a human carcinoma. Cancer Cell 1, 445–457 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e803">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Aguirre Ghiso, J. A. Inhibition of FAK signaling activated by urokinase receptor induces dormancy in human carcinoma cells in vivo. Oncogene 21, 2513–2524 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e806">25</a></sup>. Proliferating (T-HEp3) cells obtained from tumours and kept in culture reprogramme into a dormant/quiescent phenotype (D-HEp3 cells) after prolonged passaging <i>in vitro</i>. However, this dormant phenotype is not manifested <i>in vitro</i> but it is observed only after injection of D-HEp3 cells <i>in vivo</i> in nude mice s.c. or in the chicken embryo chorioallantoic membrane (CAM). In these <i>in vivo</i> settings the dormant phenotype of D-HEp3 cells can persist for months before reactivation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Adam, A. P. et al. Computational identification of a p38SAPK-regulated transcription factor network required for tumor cell quiescence. Cancer Res. 69, 5664–5672 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e823">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bragado, P. et al. TGF-beta2 dictates disseminated tumour cell fate in target organs through TGF-beta-RIII and p38alpha/beta signalling. Nat. Cell Biol. 15, 1351–1361 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e826">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Aguirre-Ghiso, J. A., Ossowski, L. &amp; Rosenbaum, S. K. Green fluorescent protein tagging of extracellular signal-regulated kinase and p38 pathways reveals novel dynamics of pathway activation during primary and metastatic growth. Cancer Res. 64, 7336–7345 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e829">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Ossowski, L. &amp; Reich, E. Changes in malignant phenotype of a human carcinoma conditioned by growth environment. Cell 33, 323–333 (1983)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e832">26</a></sup>. We compared the expression profiles of deeply quiescent D-HEp3 cells that form small nodules that do not change in size <i>in vivo</i> or proliferative T-HEp3 cells that form growing tumour masses <i>in vivo</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Adam, A. P. et al. Computational identification of a p38SAPK-regulated transcription factor network required for tumor cell quiescence. Cancer Res. 69, 5664–5672 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e841">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Aguirre-Ghiso, J. A., Ossowski, L. &amp; Rosenbaum, S. K. Green fluorescent protein tagging of extracellular signal-regulated kinase and p38 pathways reveals novel dynamics of pathway activation during primary and metastatic growth. Cancer Res. 64, 7336–7345 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e844">20</a></sup> and found that <i>NR2F1</i> was induced approximately threefold in the former cells (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>—see also methods for access to full expression profiles). This was corroborated by immunohistochemistry (IHC), immunofluorescence (IF; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 1a</a>) and western blot (WB), where nuclear NR2F1 protein was three- to fourfolds higher in D-HEp3 cells than in T-HEp3 cells (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>). This was also true for other genes in the RA pathway (RARβ)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Huang, S. et al. ZNF423 is critically required for retinoic acid-induced differentiation and is a marker of neuroblastoma outcome. Cancer Cell 15, 328–340 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e864">27</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 1b</a>). In two additional aggressive HNSCC cell lines, FaDu and SQ20B<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Bragado, P. et al. Analysis of marker-defined HNSCC subpopulations reveals a dynamic regulation of tumor initiating properties. PLoS ONE 7, e29974 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e875">28</a></sup>, NR2F1 protein and mRNA levels were barely detectable (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>). Thus, NR2F1 is downregulated in T-HEp3, FaDu and SQ20B HNSCC cells <i>in vitro</i> and tumours <i>in vivo</i> (T-HEp3) when compared with dormant D-HEp3 cells <i>in vitro</i> and dormant nodules <i>in vivo</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title=" NR2F1 and RA responsive genes in quiescent HEp3 cells."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: NR2F1 and RA responsive genes in quiescent HEp3 cells.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms7170/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms7170/MediaObjects/41467_2015_Article_BFncomms7170_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig1" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms7170/MediaObjects/41467_2015_Article_BFncomms7170_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure 1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="777"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Upper panel: RNA from sorted GFP-positive T-HEp3 (proliferative) and D-HEp3 (quiescent) cells grown in CAMs for 7 days was expression profiled using the Affymetrix platform. The heat map shows the Log<sub>2</sub>-fold mRNA level change normalized to the levels in proliferative HEp3 cells. Tumours (<i>n</i>=5) were collected and RNA pooled and run in triplicate Affymetrix HG-U133-PLUS2 arrays. Lower panel: QPCR for NR2F1 (T-HEp3 tumours <i>n</i>=12, D-HEp3 tumours <i>n</i>=15) and RARβ2 (T-HEp3 tumours <i>n</i>=9, D-HEp3 tumours <i>n</i>=6) mRNA in T- and D-HEp3 cells grown in CAMs for 7 day. (<b>b</b>) IHC for NR2F1. Upper panels, T-HEp3 tumours and D-HEp3 nodules sectioned and stained by IHC against NR2F1. Insets, higher magnification of replicate T- or D-HEp3 tumours. Scale bars, 50 μm. Lower left panel: QPCR for NR2F1 mRNA in D, D-HEp3; F, FaDu; SQ, SQ20B; T, T-HEp3 cells in the presence or absence of 10% FBS. <i>n</i>=3 RNA replicates per condition. Lower right panel: nuclear fraction lysates of the indicated cell lines IB for NR2F1. LaminB1, loading control. Three independent experiments. Scale bars, 50 μm. (<b>c</b>) Single-cell expression profiling on bone marrow DTCs. Left panel: BM DTCS were isolated from patients with NED (<i>n</i>=4 patients) and patient with ADV (<i>n</i>=6 patients). Single-cell gene expressions were analysed by Agilent 4 × 44 K Human Microarrays, and NR2F1 and TGFβ2 mRNA levels were compared between both groups. Right panel: percentage of DTCs with high levels of NR2F1 mRNA levels were plotted for NED patients or ADV patients. <i>n</i>=number of DTCs. <i>P</i> values were obtained by Mann–Whitney test. (<b>d</b>) NR2F1 function in D-HEp3 cell dormancy. Left panel: Control, NR2F1- or p38α-depleted D-HEp3 cells were inoculated into CAMs, and 4 days later the total tumour cells were counted. Right panel: IHC for Ki67 in siControl or siNR2F1 tumour sections. <i>n</i>=5 tumours per group. (<b>e</b>) QPCR for HES-1, p27, p16, p15 and cyclinD1 genes from siControl or siNR2F1 tumour RNA samples. Unless stated, all data are representative of at least triplicate independent experiments±s.d., <i>n</i>=3 RNA replicates per condition unpaired <i>t</i>-test, *<i>P</i>&lt;0.05, ***<i>P</i>&lt;0.0005. Mann–Whitney test was used for <i>in vivo</i> experiments.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms7170/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 1" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To determine the function of NR2F1 in quiescent D-HEp3 cells <i>in vivo</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Adam, A. P. et al. Computational identification of a p38SAPK-regulated transcription factor network required for tumor cell quiescence. Cancer Res. 69, 5664–5672 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e988">3</a></sup>, we performed <i>NR2F1</i> knockdown via short interfering RNA (siRNA) and found that NR2F1 promoted D-HEp3 cell exit from dormancy and tumour growth, comparable to a siRNA to p38α, as shown for other TFs in the p38α/β-regulated network<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Adam, A. P. et al. Computational identification of a p38SAPK-regulated transcription factor network required for tumor cell quiescence. Cancer Res. 69, 5664–5672 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e995">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bragado, P. et al. TGF-beta2 dictates disseminated tumour cell fate in target organs through TGF-beta-RIII and p38alpha/beta signalling. Nat. Cell Biol. 15, 1351–1361 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e998">6</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig1">Fig. 1d</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 1c</a>); no differences were observed in potency of phenotype between siNR2F1 and sip38α. Exit from dormancy coincided with downregulation of cell cycle inhibitors such as p16, p27, p15 and HES-1, all genes involved in quiescence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Florenes, V. A. et al. TGF-beta mediated G1 arrest in a human melanoma cell line lacking p15INK4B: evidence for cooperation between p21Cip1/WAF1 and p27Kip1. Oncogene 13, 2447–2457 (1996)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1009">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Sang, L., Coller, H. A. &amp; Roberts, J. M. Control of the reversibility of cellular quiescence by the transcriptional repressor HES1. Science 321, 1095–1100 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1012">30</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig1">Fig. 1e</a>). Further, NR2F1 depletion also induced upregulation of cyclinD1 levels and Ki67 staining indicative of G0 exit.</p><p>To test the potential human implications of these findings, we next tested whether NR2F1 was re-expressed in prostate cancer DTCs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Chery, L. et al. Characterization of single disseminated prostate cancer cells reveals tumor cell heterogeneity and identifies dormancy associated pathways. Oncotarget 5, 9939–9951 (2014)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1022">31</a></sup>. We chose prostate cancer because this cancer type is known to undergo prolonged dormancy phases and because <i>NR2F1</i> is commonly downregulated in prostate primary tumours<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Grasso, C. S. et al. The mutational landscape of lethal castration-resistant prostate cancer. Nature 487, 239–243 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1029">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Taylor, B. S. et al. Integrative genomic profiling of human prostate cancer. Cancer Cell 18, 11–22 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1032">16</a></sup>, but may become upregulated after hormonal ablation, which is thought to lead to residual disease dormancy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Thompson, V. C. et al. A gene signature identified using a mouse model of androgen receptor-dependent prostate cancer predicts biochemical relapse in human disease. Int. J. Cancer 131, 662–672 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1036">18</a></sup>. To this end, we compared individual prostate cancer DTCs isolated via EpCAM marking from the bone marrow of post-radical prostatectomy patients with no evidence of disease (NED—dormant disease) or advanced proliferative disease (ADV). NED patients showed undetectable PSA level (&lt;0.1 ng ml<sup>−1</sup>) 7–18 years after prostatectomy. ADV patients showed disease progression with failed treatment or existing distant metastasis. Seven EpCAM+ individual NED cells (four patients) and 37 ADV cells (six patients) were processed for expression profiling as indicated in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Table I</a> and Experimental Procedures<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Chery, L. et al. Characterization of single disseminated prostate cancer cells reveals tumor cell heterogeneity and identifies dormancy associated pathways. Oncotarget 5, 9939–9951 (2014)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1046">31</a></sup>. When comparing NED versus ADV PCa DTCs we found that 42.8% of NED DTCs showed <i>NR2F1</i> upregulation versus 10.3% in ADV patient-derived DTCs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>). The average mRNA levels for <i>NR2F1</i> showed a trend towards upregulation (<i>P</i>&lt;0.08) in NED versus the ADV patient-derived DTCs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>). A significant difference in mRNA levels (<i>P</i>&lt;0.0117) was found for TGFβ2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bragado, P. et al. TGF-beta2 dictates disseminated tumour cell fate in target organs through TGF-beta-RIII and p38alpha/beta signalling. Nat. Cell Biol. 15, 1351–1361 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1068">6</a>) another dormancy gene we recently linked to bone marrow (BM) DTC dormancy that was also more frequently upregulated in NED versus ADV DTCs (42.8% NED versus 18% ADV; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>). We conclude that dormancy genes found upregulated in HNSCC experimental models (this study and ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bragado, P. et al. TGF-beta2 dictates disseminated tumour cell fate in target organs through TGF-beta-RIII and p38alpha/beta signalling. Nat. Cell Biol. 15, 1351–1361 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1074">6</a>) are also found upregulated in DTCs from PCa patients carrying dormant disease.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Epigenetic regulation of NR2F1 expression</h3><p>IHC analysis showed that compared with benign adjacent oral mucosa NR2F1 expression was absent or low in primary (<i>n</i>=15) or recurrent (<i>n</i>=9) HNSCC tumours (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 1d,e</a>) and patient-matched lymph node metastases (<i>n</i>=11; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>). Further, while 27% of squamous cells in benign adjacent tissues were NR2F1<sup>high</sup> (7–66% range), only 1.5±2.5% of tumour cells in primary and recurrent lesions were NR2F1<sup>high</sup>. These results show that overall NR2F1 is downregulated in tumour tissues and the intratumour heterogeneity of expression is very low (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 1d,e</a>). Primary tumour tissues showed that 67% of the tumour cells were Ki67+ and none expressed NR2F1, suggesting that proliferative tumour cells are negative for NR2F1 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 1e</a>). The overall downregulation of NR2F1 in tumour versus normal squamous tissues is in accord with the expression profiling study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Ginos, M. A. et al. Identification of a gene expression signature associated with recurrent disease in squamous cell carcinoma of the head and neck. Cancer Res. 64, 55–63 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1119">12</a></sup> where NR2F1 was downregulated in ~85% (<i>n</i>=41) of HNSCC primary tumours and in ~86% (<i>n</i>=16) local recurrences. NR2F1 mRNA and protein levels were also lower in human breast carcinoma tissues and in the breast tissue of 20-week-old MMTV-Neu and -Myc mice (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 2a–c</a>), which at this age develop pre-malignant atypical ductal hyperplasia (ADH) and ductal carcinoma in situ (DCIS)-like lesions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Husemann, Y. et al. Systemic spread is an early step in breast cancer. Cancer Cell 13, 58–68 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1132">32</a></sup>. Further, in at least three studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Richardson, A. L. et al. X chromosomal abnormalities in basal-like human breast cancer. Cancer Cell 9, 121–132 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1136">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Lu, X. et al. Predicting features of breast cancer with gene expression patterns. Breast Cancer Res. Treat. 108, 191–201 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1139">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Curtis, C. et al. The genomic and transcriptomic architecture of 2,000 breast tumours reveals novel subgroups. Nature 486, 346–352 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1142">34</a></sup> including the breast cancer TCGA showed that in ~80% tumour samples <i>NR2F1</i> was significantly downregulated versus normal tissue (for example, <i>P</i>&lt;1.06 × 10<sup>−25</sup>—TGCA data as measured using a <i>t</i>-Test—Oncomine source).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="NR2F1 expression in HNSCC tumours and epigenetic regulation."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: NR2F1 expression in HNSCC tumours and epigenetic regulation.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms7170/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms7170/MediaObjects/41467_2015_Article_BFncomms7170_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig2" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms7170/MediaObjects/41467_2015_Article_BFncomms7170_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure 2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="871"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) NR2F1 protein detection in benign adjacent oral mucosa (inset, magnification of NR2F1+ epithelium), in primary tumours (PT) and lymph node metastasis (met). Scale bars, 50 μm. Images shown are representative of <i>n</i>=6 benign adjacent tissue, 15 primary tumours, 9 recurrences and 11 metastasis. (<b>b</b>) Azacytidine treatment and NR2F1 expression in HNSCC cells. T-HEp3 (top panel) and FaDu (lower panel) cells were treated with 5-azadeoxycytidine (AZA) at the indicated concentrations for 2 days, and NR2F1 mRNA quantified by QPCR. <i>y</i> axis, AZA conditions over no AZA. ***<i>P</i>&lt;0.0005, unpaired <i>t</i>-test. <i>n</i>=3 RNA samples per condition. (<b>c</b>,<b>d</b>) H3PTMs at the NR2F1 locus in T- and D-HEp3 cells. T- and D-HEp3 cells were cultured <i>in vitro</i> followed by ChIP–qPCR analysis of the NR2F1 genomic locus for H3K4me3, H3K27ac (<b>c</b>) and H3K27me3 (<b>d</b>) histone modifications. Top scheme shows the primers used around the TSS of the NR2F1 genomic locus. Bar graphs indicate fold enrichment over input (1%), *<i>P</i>&lt;0.05, **<i>P</i>&lt;0.005, unpaired <i>t</i>-test, mean±s.d. (<i>n</i>=3). Arrows show sites with significant changes for the indicated histone modification in two independent experiments. All other data are representative of at least triplicate independent experiments. (<b>e</b>) ChIP–qPCR for H3k4me3 at the NR2F1 locus in D-HEp3 cells. After NR2F1 ablation (by siRNA, 50 nM) cultured cells were subjected to ChIP–qPCR for H3K4me3 at the NR2F1 locus. Arrows show sites with significant changes for the indicated histone modification in two independent experiments. *<i>P</i>&lt;0.05, unpaired <i>t</i>-test, mean±s.d. (<i>n</i>=3). All other data are representative of at least triplicate independent experiments±s.d.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms7170/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 2" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We next tested whether NR2F1 might be downregulated by DNA promoter methylation. Treatment of T-HEp3 and FaDu cells that express negligible levels of NR2F1 with 5-azadeoxycytidine (5-Aza-C), strongly increased <i>NR2F1</i> mRNA expression by more than threefold without any toxicity (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 2d</a>). However, analysis of DNA promoter methylation showed no differences in CpG island methylation between T-HEp3 and D-HEp3 cells (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 2e</a>). Chromatin immunoprecipitation (ChIP) analysis covering a region spanning −10 to +3 Kb of the transcription start site (TSS) of the <i>NR2F1</i> gene revealed that the H3K4me3 and H3K27ac transcriptional activation marks are highly enriched at the <i>NR2F1</i> TSS in D-HEp3 as compared with T-HEp3 cells (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>). In contrast, the H3K27me3 repressive mark was enriched in the promoter of <i>NR2F1</i> in T-HEp3 cells (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>). These changes in H3-PTMs correlated with the <i>NR2F1</i> mRNA levels in T- versus D-HEp3 HNSCC cells. ChIP analysis for the same H3-PTMs mentioned above in D-HEp3 cells with and without <i>NR2F1</i> knockdown showed that on its own promoter at least H3K4me3 seems to be regulated by NR2F1 (+1 position; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig2">Fig. 2d</a>). We conclude that DNA promoter methylation in regions not covered by our analysis and/or changes in histone-H3-PTMs and transcriptional activation contribute to NR2F1 silencing in proliferative T-HEp3 cells.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">NR2F1 regulates SOX9 and RARβ during dormancy</h3><p>TF network analysis of gene profiles regulated by p38α/β<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Adam, A. P. et al. Computational identification of a p38SAPK-regulated transcription factor network required for tumor cell quiescence. Cancer Res. 69, 5664–5672 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1290">3</a></sup> identified 40 co-regulated genes with predicted or validated binding elements for <i>NR2F1</i> in their promoters (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 3a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Table II</a>). Ten of these genes contained both <i>NR2F1</i> and RARβ (a target of NR2F1 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Lin, B. et al. Orphan receptor COUP-TF is required for induction of retinoic acid receptor beta, growth inhibition, and apoptosis by retinoic acid in cancer cells. Mol. Cell. Biol. 20, 957–970 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1306">35</a>)) binding sites. These genes were upregulated &gt;2.8-fold in D-HEp3 cells both in culture and <i>in vivo</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>). We focused on SOX9 because its promoter is bound by NR2F1 and RARβ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Rada-Iglesias, A. et al. Epigenomic annotation of enhancers predicts transcriptional regulators of human neural crest. Cell Stem Cell 11, 633–648 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1319">10</a></sup> and it is a RA- and p38α/β-responsive TF<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Adam, A. P. et al. Computational identification of a p38SAPK-regulated transcription factor network required for tumor cell quiescence. Cancer Res. 69, 5664–5672 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1323">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Afonja, O. et al. RAR agonists stimulate SOX9 gene expression in breast cancer cell lines: evidence for a role in retinoid-mediated growth inhibition. Oncogene 21, 7850–7860 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1326">36</a></sup>, linked to growth inhibition and differentiation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Drivdahl, R. et al. Suppression of growth and tumorigenicity in the prostate tumor cell line M12 by overexpression of the transcription factor SOX9. Oncogene 23, 4584–4593 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1331">37</a></sup>. Accordingly, overexpression in T-HEp3 cells with an active MKK6 mutant (p38 activator), upregulated SOX9, <i>NR2F1</i> and RARβ mRNAs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 3b</a>). Further, NR2F1 overexpression in T-HEp3 cells greatly increased the activity of a SOX9-luciferase reporter (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 3c</a>), suggesting that SOX9 is a direct target of NR2F1. Finally, we show that as in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a> knockdown of <i>NR2F1</i> reduced the presence of H3K4me3 marks in the promoters of SOX9 and RARβ in dormant D-HEp3 cells (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 3d</a>) without affecting H3K27me3 marks in any tested promoter (not shown). Thus, NR2F1 is in part responsible for inducing active transcription H3-PTMs in its own promoter and that of its target genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 3d</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Identification of NR2F1 and RARβ targets."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Identification of NR2F1 and RARβ targets.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms7170/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms7170/MediaObjects/41467_2015_Article_BFncomms7170_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig3" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms7170/MediaObjects/41467_2015_Article_BFncomms7170_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure 3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="780"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Heat map for the 10 genes that share NR2F1 and RARβ-binding elements. Log2 gene expression changes, gree<i>n</i>, basal expression in Proliferative (P) T-HEp3 cells and increasing red intensity indicates upregulation in quiescent (Q) D-HEp3 cells. (<b>b</b>) Overexpression of NRF21 inhibits tumour growth. NR2F1, p27 and Lamin-B detection in T-HEp3 cells transfected with NR2F1 or Control plasmids (top left panels) or tested for tumour growth on CAMs for 5 days (bottom left panel). <i>n</i>=4 tumours per group. IHC on vector or NR2F1-overexpressing tumour sections for the indicated antigens (right panels). Scale bars, 50 μm. (<b>c</b>) NR2F1 inhibits tumour growth via SOX9. Left panel: T-HEp3 cells transfected with SOX9 or control siRNAs (50 nM) were re-transfected with empty or NR2F1 cDNA vectors. Tumour growth was quantified 4 days later on CAMs; three independent experiments (left panel). Vector, <i>n</i>=13 tumours; siC NR2F1, <i>n</i>=15 tumours; siSOX9 vector, <i>n</i>=8 tumours; siSOX9 NR2F1, <i>n</i>=10 tumours. Right panel: p16 mRNA levels relative to tubulin in T-HEp3 cells in the indicated groups. (<b>d</b>) SOX9 RNAi promotes D-HEp3 cell proliferation. D-HEp3 cells were transfected with siRNA for SOX9 or control (siC, 50 nM). Left: tumour sections from siControl and siSOX9 groups stained for p27 by IHC. Top right: growth of siControl or siSOX9-treated D-HEp3 cells on CAMs for 4 days. Bottom right: mRNA levels for p27 and p16 after 4 days <i>in vivo</i>. <i>n</i>=5 tumours per group. Scale bars, 50 μm. (<b>e</b>) SOX9 overexpression blocks reactivation from dormancy after NR2F1 RNAi. SOX9 mRNA levels were measured by QPCR. siC vector <i>n</i>=10 tumours; siNR2F1 vector, <i>n</i>=11 tumours; siNR2F1 SOX9 <i>n</i>=11 tumours. Unless stated, all data are representative of at least triplicate independent experiments±s.d., <i>n</i>=3 RNA replicates per condition unpaired <i>t</i>-test, *<i>P</i>&lt;0.05, ***<i>P</i>&lt;0.0005. Mann–Whitney test was used for <i>in vivo</i> experiments.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms7170/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 3" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>NR2F1 overexpression mimicked dormancy in T-HEp3 cells, as it induced growth suppression, a reduction in Ki67 levels and induction of p27 and SOX9 proteins and p16 and SOX9 mRNAs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig3">Fig. 3b,c</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 3e</a>). Importantly, SOX9 was required for NR2F1-induced quiescence as RNA interference (RNAi) to SOX9 in T-HEp3 cells completely blocked NR2F1-dependent induction of growth suppression (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>). This was accompanied by a decrease in p16 levels (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>). An siRNA against SOX9 in D-HEp3 cells also promoted tumour growth and decreased p16 and p27 mRNA levels and p27 protein levels (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig3">Fig. 3d</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 3f</a>), but no changes were observed in p15 and HES-1 levels (not shown; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig3">Fig. 3d</a>). SOX9 was sufficient to induce dormancy because overexpression of a cDNA encoding human SOX9 completely reversed the pro-proliferative effects of <i>NR2F1</i> siRNA on D-HEp3 cells (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig3">Fig. 3e</a>).</p><p>Since RA regulates NR2F1 expression<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Qiu, Y., Krishnan, V., Pereira, F. A., Tsai, S. Y. &amp; Tsai, M. J. Chicken ovalbumin upstream promoter-transcription factors and their regulation. J. Steroid Biochem. Mol. Biol. 56, 81–85 (1996)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1477">38</a></sup>, we tested whether the pan-RAR ligand atRA, increased NR2F1 and its target genes. We found that RARβ, SOX9 and <i>NR2F1</i> itself were induced between 1.3- and 4-fold in T-HEp3 and FaDu cells, in response to 48-h treatment with atRA (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 4a</a>). <i>NR2F1</i> depletion in T-HEp3 cells reduced atRA-induced upregulation of RARβ and SOX9 and even basal levels of SOX9 were further reduced upon <i>NR2F1</i> and RARβ knockdown (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 4b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="AzaC+atRA and NR2F1-induced reprogramming."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: AzaC+atRA and NR2F1-induced reprogramming.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms7170/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms7170/MediaObjects/41467_2015_Article_BFncomms7170_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig4" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms7170/MediaObjects/41467_2015_Article_BFncomms7170_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure 4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="603"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) T-HEp3 cells treated with 5 nM of AzaC or PBS for 48 h in media+charcolized serum were washed and stimulated with atRA (2 μM) or DMSO for 48 h in serum-free media. NR2F1, RARβ and SOX9 mRNA levels were measured by QPCR. *<i>P</i>&lt;0.05. (<b>b</b>) T-HEp3 cells were treated as in <b>a</b>. Then, cells were grown in DMEM, 10% FBS for 3 days without drugs and RARβ mRNA levels measured by QPCR. Pre-reprogramming, 4 days of Aza+atRA treatment (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 4f</a>); post reprogramming, 3 days after Aza+atRA treatment. *<i>P</i>&lt;0.05. (<b>c</b>) NR2F1, RARβ and SOX9 mRNA levels in T-HEp3 cells transfected with siControl or siNR2F1 (50 nM) treated as in <b>a</b> and left untreated for 3 days. *<i>P</i>&lt;0.05. (<b>d</b>) T-HEp3 cells were stimulated with atRA (2μM) or DMSO inoculated on CAMs (150 × 10<sup>3</sup> per animal), and tumour growth scored on day 4. <i>n</i>=4, *<i>P</i>&lt;0.05. (<b>e</b>) Sections from tumours treated as in <b>d</b> were probed for RARβ mRNA levels or stained for the indicated antigens. ***<i>P</i>&lt;0.0005. Scale bars, 15 and 75 μm. (<b>f</b>) T-HEp3 cells transfected with siRNAs for NR2F1, SOX9 or control were stimulated with atRA (2 μM) or DMSO and then inoculated into CAMs (1.5 × 10<sup>5</sup> cells per embryo) as in <b>d</b>. *<i>P</i>&lt;0.05. siC DMSO group, <i>n</i>=3 tumours; siC atRA group, <i>n</i>=5 tumours; siNR2F1 atRA group, <i>n</i>=5 tumours; siSOX9 atRA group, <i>n</i>=4 tumours. (<b>g</b>) Quantification (left) of H2B-GFP label retaining T-HEp3 cells <i>in vivo</i> after treatment as in <b>a,</b> except that 3 days before the treatment cells were induced with doxycycline to induce H2B-GFP; ***<i>P</i>&lt;0.0001. Right: phase contrast (top) and GFP channel (bottom) images of H2B-GFP tumour explants after gentle mechanical mincing. Unless stated, all data are representative of at least triplicate independent experiments. <i>y</i>=percentage of positive green cells in four fields. <i>n</i>=6 tumours per group. Scale bars, 45 μm. Unless stated, all data are representative of at least triplicate independent experiments±s.d., <i>n</i>=3 RNA replicates per condition unpaired <i>t</i>-test. Mann–Whitney test was used for <i>in vivo</i> experiments.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms7170/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 4" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We next explored the effect of 5-Aza-C on NR2F1 induction of target genes. Both RARβ and SOX9 induction in T-HEp3 and FaDu cells was strongly enhanced by the treatment (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 4c,d</a>). Interestingly, NR2F1 overexpression was not sufficient to upregulate RARβ mRNA (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 4e</a>) but pretreatment with 5-Aza-C followed by NR2F1 overexpression now made the T-HEp3 cells permissive to RARβ upregulation by exogenous NR2F1 above the effect of 5-Aza-C alone (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 4e</a>). Accordingly, in T-HEp3 cells treated with a combination of low-dose of Aza-C (5 nM) + atRA (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 4f</a>), <i>NR2F1</i>, SOX9 and RARβ mRNAs were significantly upregulated (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>).</p><p>The effect of atRA on RARβ was transient and its expression returned to pre-induction levels 3 days after atRA washout (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>). In contrast, when cells were pre-treated with AzaC (5 nM) followed by atRA (2 μM), the induced levels of RARβ were maintained even after atRA removal for 3 days (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>). The AzaC + atRA treatment was also able to induce RARβ expression across various cancer cell line subtypes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 5a</a>). Importantly, the sustained expression of RARβ and SOX9 in these conditions required NR2F1 expression as tested in T-HEp3, MCF-7 and SQ20B cells (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 5a</a>). These results argue that in carcinoma cell lines from different tissues, AzaC-plus-atRA-induced gene expression is durable and dependent on NR2F1.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">AtRA and 5-Aza-C treatment induces dormancy</h3><p>To expand the above experiments, we queried whether treatment of T-HEp3 cells with atRA would induce the key hallmarks of reprogramming into quiescence <i>in vivo</i>. Treatment with atRA for 5 days resulted in inhibition of tumour growth <i>in vivo</i> using the chicken chorioallantoic membrane assays for rapid <i>in vivo</i> readout<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Aguirre-Ghiso, J. A., Ossowski, L. &amp; Rosenbaum, S. K. Green fluorescent protein tagging of extracellular signal-regulated kinase and p38 pathways reveals novel dynamics of pathway activation during primary and metastatic growth. Cancer Res. 64, 7336–7345 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1680">20</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig4">Fig. 4d</a>). Growth inhibition was with no apparent change in apoptosis or senescence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 5b</a>). Analysis of the tumour tissue from CAMs showed that atRA treatment caused a marked upregulation of dormancy genes. These included upregulation of RARβ and p21 mRNAs and NR2F1 and SOX9 proteins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig4">Fig. 4e</a>) and downregulation of Ki67 and P-ERK1/2 levels (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig4">Fig. 4e</a>), suggesting a G0 arrest. Treatment with atRA also increased the phosphorylation of ATF2, a downstream p38 target (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig4">Fig. 4e</a>) suggesting a low ERK/p38 activity ratio<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Aguirre-Ghiso, J. A., Ossowski, L. &amp; Rosenbaum, S. K. Green fluorescent protein tagging of extracellular signal-regulated kinase and p38 pathways reveals novel dynamics of pathway activation during primary and metastatic growth. Cancer Res. 64, 7336–7345 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1700">20</a></sup>. Importantly, in T-HEp3 cells RNAi to SOX9 and NR2F1 were sufficient to eliminate the growth inhibitory effect of atRA <i>in vivo</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig4">Fig. 4f</a>). However, as expected (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>), atRA-treated T-HEp3 cells that were growth inhibited for 1 week, escaped this inhibition by atRA after a second week <i>in vivo</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 5c</a>).</p><p>We next tested whether the persistent effect of combined 5-Aza-C+atRA treatment in culture (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>) could induce prolonged T-HEp3 quiescence state <i>in vivo</i>. We used a Tet-ON-inducible H2B-GFP-tagged T-HEp3 line where doxycycline (DOX) induces H2B-GFP and upon de-induction and DNA replication only cells that are quiescent are H2B-GFP-positive. H2B-GFP-tagged T-HEp3 cells treated only <i>in vitro</i> with 5-Aza-C+atRA for 4 days were then followed on CAMs for 6 days (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig4">Fig. 4g</a>). In this sequence the 5-Aza-C+atRA treatment induced a persistent dormant phenotype that lasted for at least 2 weeks <i>in vivo</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 5d</a>). This correlated with the percentage of H2B-GFP-positive cells in AzaC+atRA-treated cells that was approximately fivefold greater at 6 days than in vehicle-treated cells (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig4">Fig. 4g</a>). Together, these data argue that restoring <i>NR2F1</i>, RARβ and SOX9 expression during the AzaC+atRA reprogramming phase could induce a sustained (at least 2 weeks) dormant phenotype.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">NR2F1 regulates expression of pluripotency genes</h3><p>NR2F1 regulates lineage commitment and differentiation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Rada-Iglesias, A. et al. Epigenomic annotation of enhancers predicts transcriptional regulators of human neural crest. Cell Stem Cell 11, 633–648 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1757">10</a></sup> and it was shown to block iPS reprogramming<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Onder, T. T. et al. Chromatin-modifying enzymes as modulators of reprogramming. Nature 483, 598–602 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1761">4</a></sup>. However, whether NR2F1 affects pluripotency gene expression upon dormancy onset is unknown. We found that both NANOG and SOX2 mRNAs were significantly upregulated in dormant BM-HEp3 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bragado, P. et al. TGF-beta2 dictates disseminated tumour cell fate in target organs through TGF-beta-RIII and p38alpha/beta signalling. Nat. Cell Biol. 15, 1351–1361 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1764">6</a> and D-HEp3 cells over the proliferative T-HEp3 and Lu-HEp3 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bragado, P. et al. TGF-beta2 dictates disseminated tumour cell fate in target organs through TGF-beta-RIII and p38alpha/beta signalling. Nat. Cell Biol. 15, 1351–1361 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1767">6</a>)) cells (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig5">Fig. 5a</a>). Furthermore, NANOG and SOX2 mRNAs were strongly induced by the AzaC+atRA treatment and siRNAs to <i>NR2F1</i> completely eliminated this induction (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig5">Fig. 5b</a>). The induction of these pluripotency genes by the Aza+atRA reprogramming protocol was not correlated with tumour-initiating capacity as this reprogramming inhibited the ability of T-HEp3 cells to form tumour spheres in stem cell media (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 5e</a>). NR2F1 also inhibited tumour growth of MMTV-Myc mammary tumour cells, where overexpression of NR2F1 significantly reduced tumorigenicity in mice and tumour sphere formation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig5">Fig. 5c,d</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 6a</a>). We conclude that induction of pluripotency genes by NR2F1 in dormant cancer cells is associated with a growth-suppressive or quiescent behaviour and not with immediate tumour-initiating capacity.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="NR2F1 and tumour-initiating properties."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: NR2F1 and tumour-initiating properties.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms7170/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms7170/MediaObjects/41467_2015_Article_BFncomms7170_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig5" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms7170/MediaObjects/41467_2015_Article_BFncomms7170_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure 5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="491"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Expression of SOX2 and NANOG in proliferative versus dormant HEp3 sublines. Cells were grown in complete medium and RNA was extracted and converted to cDNA. SOX2 and NANOG mRNA levels were measured by QPCR. ***<i>P</i>&lt;0.0005, *<i>P</i>&lt;0.05, **<i>P</i>&lt;0.005, unpaired <i>t</i>-test. Lung: a line derived from HEp3 lung DTCs (proliferative). BM: a line derived from HEp3 BM DTCs (dormant). (<b>b</b>) AzaC+atRA treatment requires NR2F1 to induce SOX2 and NANOG. T-HEp3 cells treated with AzaC+atRA or PBS/DMSO were transfected with siControl or siNR2F1, and SOX2 and NANOG mRNAs were detected by QPCR. (<b>c</b>) NR2F1 inhibits MMTV-Myc tumour sphere formation capacity. MMTV-myc cells stably expressing an empty vector control or murine NR2F1 cDNA were inoculated orthotopically in syngeneic FvB mouse (1,000 cells per mouse). Tumour size was measured daily and the final tumour size is shown. In all panels, experiments were performed a minimum of two times. V, vector, *<i>P</i>&lt;0.05, unpaired <i>t</i>-test. <i>N</i>=6 per condition, <i>y</i>=tumour volume. (<b>d</b>) Same cells as in panel <b>c</b>, were cultured in mammosphere conditions and quantified (top left), and 8 days later cells were analysed by FACS for CD29 high and CD24-low markers (top right), and quantified and imaged (bottom left and right). Pictures show MMTV-myc mammoespheres. V, vector, ***<i>P</i>&lt;0.0005, unpaired <i>t</i> test. <i>n</i>=3 wells per condition, <i>y</i>=number of spheres per conditions. Scale bars, 200 μm. Unless stated, all data are representative of at least triplicate independent experiments±s.d. and QPCR: <i>n</i>=3 RNA replicates per condition.
