Mann–Whitney test was used for <i>in vivo</i> experiments.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms7170/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 5" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">NR2F1<sup>high</sup> cells display a global repressive chromatin state</h3><p>We showed that tumor-initiating cells display an open chromatin state (H3K27me3<sup>low</sup>, H3K9me3<sup>low</sup>, H3K4me3<sup>low</sup>), while non-tumour-initiating cells displayed a reverse profile and a more repressive or ‘bivalent’ chromatin state<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Bragado, P. et al. Analysis of marker-defined HNSCC subpopulations reveals a dynamic regulation of tumor initiating properties. PLoS ONE 7, e29974 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e1887">28</a></sup>. NR2F1 could regulate local changes in H3-PTMs in its own and SOX9 and RARβ promoters, but the global effect of NR2F1 on chromatin marks was not obvious. IHC and IF analyses showed that in contrast to T-HEp3 cells (NR2F1<sup>low</sup>), D-HEp3 cells (NR2F1<sup>high</sup>) showed a H3K9me3<sup>high</sup> and H3K27me3<sup>high</sup> profile (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig6">Fig. 6a</a>). Accordingly, the AzaC+atRA reprogramming protocol rendered T-HEp3 cells H3K27me3<sup>high</sup>/H3K9me3<sup>high</sup>/H3K4me3<sup>low</sup>/NR2F1<sup>high</sup>/SOX9<sup>high</sup> and quiescent <i>in vivo</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig6">Fig. 6b,c</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Figs 5d and 6b</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig4">Fig. 4g</a>); this effect was evident up to 8 days after drugs washout when these tumour cells are still nonproliferative. Importantly, basal H3-PTMs (K9me3 and K27me3) levels and the global changes in H3-PTMs induced by the reprogramming were NR2F1-dependent (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig6">Fig. 6d</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="NR2F1 and global H3-PTMs."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6: NR2F1 and global H3-PTMs.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms7170/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms7170/MediaObjects/41467_2015_Article_BFncomms7170_Fig6_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig6" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms7170/MediaObjects/41467_2015_Article_BFncomms7170_Fig6_HTML.jpg" alt="figure 6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="706"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) H3K27me3 and H3K9me3 levels in T- and D-HEp3 cells. D-HEp3 and T-HEp3 cells grown on coverslips were fixed with 4% PFA and stained with anti-H3K27me3 and -H3K9me3 antibodies (left panels); representative images are shown. Quantification (right panel) was performed after setting up fixed arbitrary units of immunoflourescence intensity. High mean intensity (MIF) fluorescence was scored using Metamorph for each group. ***<i>P</i>&lt;0.0005, unpaired <i>t</i>-test. Scale bars, 40 μm. (<b>b</b>) Effect of AzaC+atRa treatment on H3K27me3 and H3K9me3 levels. T-HEp3 cells grown <i>in vitro</i> were treated with AzaC+atRA or PBS/DMSO as described in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a> were stained for the indicated markers and percent of high MFI cells scored. *<i>P</i>&lt;0.05, unpaired <i>t</i>-test. (<b>c</b>) AzaC+atRa treatment renders T-HEp3 cells [H3K27me3/H3K9me3/NR2F1/SOX9]<sup>high</sup> <i>in vivo</i>. T-HEp3 cells were treated with AzaC+atRa as described in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 4f</a>, and 1 day after washout cells were inoculated into CAMs (150 × 10<sup>3</sup> cells per embryo). One week later, tumours were collected and histological sections were stained by IHC using the indicated antibodies. Scale bars, 50 μm. Insets show higher magnification views. Scale bars, 50 μm, insets=15 μm. (<b>d</b>) NR2F1 is required to induce high H3K9me3 and H3K27me3 levels after AzaC+atRa treatment. T-HEp3 cells were transfected with siControl or siNR2F1 and then treated with AzaC+atRA or PBS/DMSO as described in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 4f</a>. Three days after reprogramming, cells were fixed and stained for the indicated markers. ***<i>P</i>&lt;0.0005, **<i>P</i>&lt;0.005, *<i>P</i>&lt;0.05, unpaired <i>t</i>-test. Unless stated, all data are representative of at least triplicate independent experiments±s.d. For <b>a</b>,<b>b</b>,<b>d</b>, <i>n</i>=200 cells, <i>y</i>=percentage of cells with high MIF.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms7170/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 6" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">NR2F1 regulates residual tumour cell fate post-tumour surgery</h3><p>We next studied whether NR2F1 could dictate DTC fate. After primary tumour surgery, HEp3 cells form overt metastases in lungs and lymph nodes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Aguirre-Ghiso, J. A., Ossowski, L. &amp; Rosenbaum, S. K. Green fluorescent protein tagging of extracellular signal-regulated kinase and p38 pathways reveals novel dynamics of pathway activation during primary and metastatic growth. Cancer Res. 64, 7336–7345 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2032">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Ossowski, L., Russo, H., Gartner, M. &amp; Wilson, E. L. Growth of a human carcinoma (HEp3) in nude mice: rapid and efficient metastasis. J. Cell. Physiol. 133, 288–296 (1987)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2035">39</a></sup>. In contrast, in the bone marrow and spleen DTCs are detectable, but overt metastases never develop<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bragado, P. et al. TGF-beta2 dictates disseminated tumour cell fate in target organs through TGF-beta-RIII and p38alpha/beta signalling. Nat. Cell Biol. 15, 1351–1361 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2039">6</a></sup>. In addition, at least 2 months after surgery, local recurrences develop in &lt;17% of animals. Inducible knockdown of <i>NR2F1</i> in Tet-ON-sh-NR2F1-miR-T-HEp3 cells was &gt;60% after DOX treatment (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 6c,d</a>), without changes in its close related homologue <i>NR2F2</i> levels (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 6d</a>). Strikingly, sh-NR2F1-miR induction resulted in vigorous development of local tumour recurrences (33%; <i>n</i>=15), while in mice without DOX treatment recurrences were fewer (17% <i>n</i>=11) and developed later (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig7">Fig. 7a</a>). After take, sh-NR2F1-miR ‘ON’ tumours also grew much faster than the sh-NR2F1-miR ‘OFF’ controls reaching a much larger volume (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 6e</a>). We also found that AzaC+atRA-induced tumour growth inhibition was blocked by the activation of the sh-NR2F1-miR in HEp3 cells (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig7">Fig. 7b</a>). Thus, residual tumour cells may remain dormant and express high levels of NR2F1. Accordingly, residual solitary tumour cells in surgery margins showed upregulation of NR2F1 and SOX9, while the proliferation marker P-H3 was absent (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig7">Fig. 7a</a>). These data argue that dormant NR2F1<sup>hi</sup>/ SOX9<sup>hi</sup>/P-H3<sup>lo</sup> residual solitary tumour cells exist in surgery margins.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Effect of NR2F1 knockdown on locoregional and distant recurrences."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 7: Effect of NR2F1 knockdown on locoregional and distant recurrences.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms7170/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms7170/MediaObjects/41467_2015_Article_BFncomms7170_Fig7_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig7" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms7170/MediaObjects/41467_2015_Article_BFncomms7170_Fig7_HTML.jpg" alt="figure 7" loading="lazy" width="685" height="662"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Upper panels: Tet-ON-inducible shRNAmir-NR2F1 T-HEp3 s.c. tumours that reached ~800 mm<sup>3</sup> in nude mice were surgically removed and the mice were treated with (25 mg kg<sup>−1</sup> DOX, every 48 h, <i>n</i>=15) or without (<i>N</i>=12, middle panel) DOX (right panel). Flat green or red lines on the <i>x</i> axis are all animals without recurrences. Lower left panel: skin sections in surgery margins (48 h after surgeries) were analysed by IF. T-HEp3 cells were identified by vimentin staining (green). NR2F1, SOX9 and P-H3 (red) were stained with specific antibodies. Graph, % of positive cells for each marker per field. RTC, single residual tumour cell; TM, tumour mass; <i>n</i>=total number of cells counted in two to five animals. The lower right panel shows representative pictures for each antigen. Inset: higher magnification of cells in the red channel (grey scale pseudocolour). TM, <i>n</i>=3 animals, RTCs, <i>n</i>=3 animals; for SOX9: TM, <i>n</i>=3 animals, RTCs, <i>n</i>=5 animals; for P-H3: TM, <i>n</i>=2 animals, RTCs, <i>n</i>=3 animals. Scale bars, 10 μm. (<b>b</b>) NR2F1 knockdown and AzaC+atRA(A/A)-induced dormancy. T-HEp3 cells treated <i>in vitro</i> with PBS/DMSO (c, control) or A/A treatment were inoculated into nude mice (<i>n</i>=5) and then treated as in <b>a</b>; 12 days later, tumour growth was measured. *<i>P</i>&lt;0.05, unpaired <i>t</i>-test. (<b>c</b>) Effect of NR2F1 knockdown on spleen DTCs. DTCs isolated from the spleen of the same animals in <b>a</b> were detected by Alu-QPCR as described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bragado, P. et al. TGF-beta2 dictates disseminated tumour cell fate in target organs through TGF-beta-RIII and p38alpha/beta signalling. Nat. Cell Biol. 15, 1351–1361 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2157">6</a></sup> Mann–Whitney test <i>P</i>&lt;0.05. SPLEN DTCs: <i>n</i>=14 mice control, <i>n</i>=11 mice shNR2F1, <i>y</i>=<i>C</i><sub>t</sub> values in triplicate per mice. (<b>d</b>) Effect of NR2F1 knockdown on lung DTCs. The same animals in <b>a</b> were used to detect DTCs from the lung by Alu-QPCR. Mann–Whitney test <i>P</i>&lt;0.05. Lung DCTS: <i>n</i>=11 mice control, <i>n</i>=14 mice shNR2F1, <i>y</i>=<i>C</i><sub>t</sub> values in triplicate per mice. (<b>e</b>) Effect of NR2F1 knockdown on BM DTCs. BM aspirates from same animals in <b>a</b> were used to detect BM DTCs by Alu-QPCR. Mann–Whitney test <i>P</i>&lt;0.05. BM DTCs: <i>n</i>=10 mice control, <i>n</i>=12 mice shNR2F1 <i>y</i>=<i>C</i><sub>t</sub> values in triplicate per mice. Unless stated, all data are representative of at least duplicate independent experiments±s.d.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms7170/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 7" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Importantly, when we monitored DTC burden after primary tumour surgery using human-specific <i>Alu</i>-sequence qPCR, we found that by reducing NR2F1 levels (upon DOX treatment) we could increase the median number of tumour cells in spleens and lungs by two to fivefold (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig7">Fig. 7c,d</a>). Because this is performed after primary tumour surgery and there is no more cell dissemination, this result suggests that the observed increase in the number of tumour cells/organ may be because of re-awakening of dormant DTCs after NR2F1 knockdown. The detection of 0.5–6 × 10<sup>6</sup> lung tumour cells is correlated with the presence of metastasis as reported previously at these time points<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bragado, P. et al. TGF-beta2 dictates disseminated tumour cell fate in target organs through TGF-beta-RIII and p38alpha/beta signalling. Nat. Cell Biol. 15, 1351–1361 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2244">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Ossowski, L., Russo, H., Gartner, M. &amp; Wilson, E. L. Growth of a human carcinoma (HEp3) in nude mice: rapid and efficient metastasis. J. Cell. Physiol. 133, 288–296 (1987)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2247">39</a></sup>. Interestingly, in the bone marrow where HEp3 DTCs do not proliferate, the <i>NR2F1</i> knockdown caused a reduction in BM DTC burden (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig7">Fig. 7e</a>). This may be due to NR2F1-induced survival because knockdown of this TF in the BM-HEp3 cells that express higher <i>NR2F1</i> mRNA levels than the proliferative counterparts, also resulted in less dormant tumour cells recovered <i>in vivo</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 6f,g</a>).</p><p>We next tested the function of NANOG, which is downregulated upon NR2F1 depletion in BM-HEp3 cells (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 6f</a>). To our surprise, unlike <i>NR2F1</i> knockdown, NANOG knockdown reactivated dormant BM-HEp3 cells <i>in vivo</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 6h</a>). It is possible that NR2F1-induced NANOG in BM-HEp3 regulates quiescence—and that NR2F1 regulates directly target genes involved in survival. Thus, depending on the organ microenvironment, NR2F1 might fulfil quiescence and/or survival functions and the former may be executed by NANOG in the BM.</p><p>Finally, we tested the link between atRA, NR2F1 and TGFβ2 signalling because we found that in the bone marrow TGFβ2 induces dormancy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bragado, P. et al. TGF-beta2 dictates disseminated tumour cell fate in target organs through TGF-beta-RIII and p38alpha/beta signalling. Nat. Cell Biol. 15, 1351–1361 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2285">6</a></sup> and this cytokine mRNA was upregulated in PCa DTCs from NED versus ADV patients. In addition, atRA is abundant in the BM where it regulates HSC self-renewal and differentiation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Ghiaur, G. et al. Regulation of human hematopoietic stem cell self-renewal by the microenvironment's control of retinoic acid signaling. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 110, 16121–16126 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2289">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Purton, L. E. et al. RARgamma is critical for maintaining a balance between hematopoietic stem cell self-renewal and differentiation. J. Exp. Med. 203, 1283–1293 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2292">41</a></sup>. Treatment of T-HEp3 cells with atRA induced TGFβ2 mRNA, but this was not mediated by NR2F1 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 6i</a>). These data support a model where microenvironmental signals propagated by RA and TGFβ2 signalling might cooperate to induce quiescence and survival of DTCs in the bone marrow.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>The role of NR2F1 in quiescence induction and cell lineage commitment<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Naka, H., Nakamura, S., Shimazaki, T. &amp; Okano, H. Requirement for COUP-TFI and II in the temporal specification of neural stem cells in CNS development. Nat. Neurosci. 11, 1014–1023 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2308">42</a></sup> might explain why it is downregulated in proliferative tumours and how its re-expression by specific microenvironments or by ‘epigenetic therapies’ induces stable dormancy in aggressive tumour cells (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig8">Fig. 8</a>). Our findings, while limited to some extent by the use of human cancer cell lines and mouse models, still appear to provide relevant information regarding human DTC dormancy. For example, <i>NR2F1</i> and TGFβ2 are more frequently upregulated in DTCs from NED than ADV PCa patients. This strongly argues that NR2F1- and TGFβ2-driven dormancy programmes may be extremely long-lived in DTCs in human bone marrow (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig8">Fig. 8</a>). Further, these prostate cancer DTCs had a significant upregulation of the whole dormancy signature identified in our dormancy model, further arguing that there may be a commonality in the mechanisms that reprogramme tumour cells into dormancy. This is also supported by our findings that the same signature predicts for longer metastasis-free periods in breast cancer patients with ER+ luminal tumours<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Kim, R. S. et al. Dormancy signatures and metastasis in estrogen receptor positive and negative breast cancer. PLoS ONE 7, e35569 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2321">7</a></sup>. In our system of BM DTC dormancy NR2F1 drives growth arrest via SOX9, RARβ and NANOG, while NR2F1 itself favours survival. In D-HEp3 cells NR2F1 drives quiescence. How these different functions are coordinated is still unknown but it may be related to how these cells are reprogrammed into dormancy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Adam, A. P. et al. Computational identification of a p38SAPK-regulated transcription factor network required for tumor cell quiescence. Cancer Res. 69, 5664–5672 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2326">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bragado, P. et al. TGF-beta2 dictates disseminated tumour cell fate in target organs through TGF-beta-RIII and p38alpha/beta signalling. Nat. Cell Biol. 15, 1351–1361 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2329">6</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-8" data-title="Integrative scheme of NR2F1 functions and the regulation of DTC fate."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig8" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 8: Integrative scheme of NR2F1 functions and the regulation of DTC fate.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms7170/figures/8" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms7170/MediaObjects/41467_2015_Article_BFncomms7170_Fig8_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig8" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms7170/MediaObjects/41467_2015_Article_BFncomms7170_Fig8_HTML.jpg" alt="figure 8" loading="lazy" width="685" height="660"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-8-desc"><p>DTCs that arrive to specific microenvironments (for example, lung and bone marrow) might integrate stress signals imposed by dissemination (p38α/β activation), by the new microenvironment and growth-restrictive signals that maintain normal organ function (atRA+p38α/β activation). These signals jointly result in NR2F1 upregulation, which can in turn induce a quiescence programme via the induction of CDK inhibitors (that is, p16). This is executed by another set of TFs that include at least SOX9 and RARβ. In lung, spleen and surgery margins, the NR2F1-regulated quiescence programme seems to be important to maintain dormancy, while in the bone marrow NR2F1 appeared to primarily regulate survival of DTCs. NR2F1 was also found to regulate the expression of the two key pluripotency genes, <i>NANOG</i> and <i>SOX2</i>. A division of labour between NANOG and NR2F1 occurs in BM DTCs where growth arrest signals are regulated by NANOG, while survival pathways are subjected to NRF21 functions. The ability of NR2F1 to coordinate these programmes long term may be because of its ability to generate global changes in histone-H3-PTMs. atRA also regulates the induction of TGFβ2 in malignant cells that further induces signals in the dormancy programme by inducing DEC2 via canonical and noncanonical TGFβ2 signalling; this is independent of NR2F1. Finally, the dormancy programme may be manipulated to favour dormancy maintenance using 5-Aza-C combined with atRA as an epigenetic therapy. This reprogramming protocol may also be used to study how dormant cells survive in quiescence and design antisurvival therapies that target quiescent DTCs.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms7170/figures/8" data-track-dest="link:Figure8 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 8" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The plasticity in NR2F1 expression suggests that specific signals might restore NR2F1<sup>high</sup> levels in disseminated disease as observed in DTCs from PCa patients who had NED from 7 to 18 years. In agreement with these findings, <i>NR2F1</i> expression was higher in prostate cancer patient’s tumours after hormonal therapy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Thompson, V. C. et al. A gene signature identified using a mouse model of androgen receptor-dependent prostate cancer predicts biochemical relapse in human disease. Int. J. Cancer 131, 662–672 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2367">18</a></sup>. Thus, microenvironmental signals or therapy may induce NR2F1 re-expression and determine the rate at which residual disease reactivates. A potential utility might be the detection of NR2F1, SOX9, RARβ and possibly NANOG in DTCs as biomarkers. These may pinpoint DTCs in a dormant state and with latent capacity to reactivate or that responded to epigenetic therapies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Tsai, H. C. et al. Transient low doses of DNA-demethylating agents exert durable antitumor effects on hematological and epithelial tumor cells. Cancer Cell 21, 430–446." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2371">43</a></sup>. These could also be tested in CTCs in M0 patients to identify precursors of dormant DTCs or re-circulating dormant tumour cells. Alternatively, as with DTCs, testing these markers on CTCs may be informative on response to therapies that activate the dormancy mechanisms.</p><p>Our data reveal something particular about NR2F1 in that in malignant cells that entered dormancy it coordinates quiescence, survival and the expression of pluripotency genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig8">Fig. 8</a>). In this dormancy context, NANOG may help to maintain the quiescence phase induced by NR2F1 upregulation. We cannot rule out that the pluripotency genes may allow DTCs to survive and avoid differentiation, senescence or at some point reactivate. The stability and also plasticity of the dormant phenotype were linked to the ability of NR2F1 to induce global repressive chromatin states as evidenced by enrichment in H3K27me3 and H3K9me3 marks upon spontaneous or Aza+atRA-induced reprogramming (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig8">Fig. 8</a>). While globally NR2F1 induces a chromatin-repressive state, the regulation by NR2F1 of H3K4me3 marks in its own and SOX9 and RARβ promoters indicate that it can also regulate local active chromatin changes.</p><p>Specific microenvironments may contain cues (for example, RA and TGFβ2) that in malignant tumour cells receptive to these signals may activate a dormancy programme. Remarkably, NR2F1 appears to execute such a programme in occult DTCs in several organs and in the surgery margins by inducing a specific combination of pluripotency (NR2F1<sup>high</sup>/[NANOG/Sox2]<sup>high</sup>) and quiescence (SOX9<sup>high</sup>/RARβ<sup>high</sup>/p27<sup>high</sup>) genes. This may explain the longer metastasis-free periods in patients carrying NR2F1<sup>high</sup> tumours (and putative NR2F1<sup>high</sup> DTCs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Kim, R. S. et al. Dormancy signatures and metastasis in estrogen receptor positive and negative breast cancer. PLoS ONE 7, e35569 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2402">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Thompson, V. C. et al. A gene signature identified using a mouse model of androgen receptor-dependent prostate cancer predicts biochemical relapse in human disease. Int. J. Cancer 131, 662–672 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2405">18</a></sup>.</p><p>Our results show that ‘epigenetic therapies’ (demethylating agents) currently used in patients<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Tsai, H. C. et al. Transient low doses of DNA-demethylating agents exert durable antitumor effects on hematological and epithelial tumor cells. Cancer Cell 21, 430–446." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2413">43</a></sup> could induce dormancy when used at low doses and combined with atRA (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#Fig8">Fig. 8</a>). Remarkably, the key genes (for example, RARβ and CDKN1A) that were induced by the p38α/β<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Adam, A. P. et al. Computational identification of a p38SAPK-regulated transcription factor network required for tumor cell quiescence. Cancer Res. 69, 5664–5672 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2420">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Kim, R. S. et al. Dormancy signatures and metastasis in estrogen receptor positive and negative breast cancer. PLoS ONE 7, e35569 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2423">7</a></sup> were also induced by demethylating agents<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Tsai, H. C. et al. Transient low doses of DNA-demethylating agents exert durable antitumor effects on hematological and epithelial tumor cells. Cancer Cell 21, 430–446." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2427">43</a></sup>. Our combination of ‘differentiation’ and ‘epigenetic’ therapies or the combination of specific transcription factors (for example, NR2F1, SOX9 and RARβ) might be a strategy to generate induced dormant cancer cells. This may provide the basis for designing new therapeutics to prevent residual disease from reactivating after tumour surgery and/or chemotherapy. Such ‘dormancy-inducing therapies’ could be used to convert residual proliferative tumour cells into dormancy and/or prevent pre-existing dormant cells from re-awakening. On the other hand, <i>in vitro</i> generation of induced dormant cancer cells or dormant DTCs obtained from different microenvironments may allow discovering the survival mechanisms that allow them to persist over time and eradicate them. BM DTCs may require NR2F1 for survival, suggesting that targeting the downstream executors of the survival programme, may be useful to eliminate DTCs in cancers that primarily target bone such as prostate cancer and ER+ luminal breast cancer. This also implies that a global single approach to target all disseminated cells found in different tissues in a patient may not be the best scenario. Perhaps a combination of drugs that specifically target the key dormancy-regulating genes in specific microenvironments might be needed.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Cell lines</h3><p>Tumorigenic (T-HEp3) HEp3 cells were derived from a lymph node metastasis from a HNSCC patient as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Ossowski, L. &amp; Reich, E. Changes in malignant phenotype of a human carcinoma conditioned by growth environment. Cell 33, 323–333 (1983)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2446">26</a></sup> and kept as patient-derived xenografts in CAMs. Dormant D-HEp3 cells were obtained by passing T-HEp3 cells for more than 40 generations <i>in vitro</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Ossowski, L. &amp; Reich, E. Changes in malignant phenotype of a human carcinoma conditioned by growth environment. Cell 33, 323–333 (1983)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2452">26</a></sup>. Dormant (BM-HEp3) and proliferative (Lu-HEp3) cells derived from bone marrow- and lung-derived T-HEp3 DTCs were described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bragado, P. et al. TGF-beta2 dictates disseminated tumour cell fate in target organs through TGF-beta-RIII and p38alpha/beta signalling. Nat. Cell Biol. 15, 1351–1361 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2456">6</a></sup>. When cultured <i>in vitro</i> all these cells were passaged in DMEM cell growth medium (Dulbeco’s modified medium with 10% of fetal bovine serum (FBS) and 100 U penicillin/0.1 mg ml<sup>−1</sup> streptomycin). The 4T1 cells are derived from a single spontaneous primary mammary tumour that arose in a wild-type BALB/c mouse<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Aslakson, C. J. &amp; Miller, F. R. Selective events in the metastatic process defined by analysis of the sequential dissemination of subpopulations of a mouse mammary tumor. Cancer Res. 52, 1399–1405 (1992)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2466">44</a></sup>. ShNR2F1miR cells were generated by TURBO-RFP-shNR2F1miR encoding lentivirus infection of T-HEp3 cells then selected with puromycin (2.5 μg ml<sup>−1</sup>). For the MMTV-myc studies, primary cultures from MMTV-myc mouse tumours<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Bosch, A. et al. Reversal by RARalpha agonist Am580 of c-Myc-induced imbalance in RARalpha/RARgamma expression during MMTV-Myc tumorigenesis. Breast Cancer Res. 14, R121 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2472">45</a></sup> were transfected with pGS5-empty or pGS5-Nr2f1. Briefly, MMTV-myc tumour cells were isolated by adding collagenase for 30 min at 37 °C. Then, suspension of cells were plated in 10 cm dishes to allow for fibrosblast to attach and the resuspended cells were treated with PBS-EDTA (2 mM) for 15 min at 37 °C to reach single-cell suspension. Finally, tumour cells were plated and grown in DMEM:F12 medium with 5% horse serum, EGF (20 ng ml<sup>−1</sup>), Hydrocortisone (0.5 mg ml<sup>−1</sup>), cholera toxin (100 ng ml<sup>−1</sup>), insulin (10 μg ml<sup>−1</sup>), penicillin/streptomycin (1 × solution). pCDNA3.1(+) was used as a selection marker by co-transfection in a ratio 10:1. G418-resistant cells were selected by cloning dilution. SQ20b, MCF-7 and FaDu cells were obtained from ATCC and were grown in the same medium as T-HEp3 cells. 4T1 and PC3 were obtained from ATCC and grown in RPMI medium with 10% of FBS.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Reprogramming protocol</h3><p>After seeding, T-HEp3 cells were grown in DMEM high-glucose media plus 10% of charcolized serum and penicillin/streptomycin and the addition of Azacytidyne (5-AzaCytidine, 5 nM) for 24 h. Next day, fresh AzaC (5 nM) was added (See <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Fig. 4f</a>). Then, media was changed for serum-free media plus atRA (2 μM) for 48 h. All these treatments are designed as pre-reprogramming step (pre-step). Subsequently, the media was changed for normal growing media without the addition of any drug (post-step) and with this started the post-reprogramming protocol.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Reagents and antibodies</h3><p>Charcoal Stripped FBS One Shot (Gibco, Life technology, NY, USA), 5-azadeoxycytidine (Sigma, MO, USA), atRA (Sigma), senescence beta galactosidase kit (Cell Signaling, MA, USA), Dual luciferase reporter system (Promega, Wisconsin, USA), trizol (Invitrogen, NY, USA), NR2F1 antibody for western blot (R&amp;D systems, MN, USA), NR2F1 antibody for IF and IHC (Abcam, MA, USA), p27, p21 and cleaved-caspase 3 (Cell signaling), Ki67 (Invitrogen) and SOX9 (Millipore, MA, USA). Human NR2F1 plasmid was a kind gift from Dr Gilles Salbert (Rennes University, France). Mouse NR2F1 plasmid was a kind gift from Dr Lorraine Gudas (Weill Cornell Medical College). All primary antibodies for IF/IHC were used at a dilution of 1:100.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Xenograft studies</h3><p>All animal studies were approved by Institutional Animal Care and Use Committees (IACUC) at the Mount Sinai School of Medicine Protocol ID: 08–0366. Tumour growth on chick embryo CAMs or Balb/c nude mice has been described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Aguirre Ghiso, J. A., Kovalski, K. &amp; Ossowski, L. Tumor dormancy induced by downregulation of urokinase receptor in human carcinoma involves integrin and MAPK signaling. J. Cell Biol. 147, 89–104 (1999)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2512">46</a></sup>. Briefly, 150 × 10<sup>3</sup> T-HEp3 cells were inoculated on the chicken embryo chorioallantoic membrane and allowed them to grow for the indicated times in each experiment (from 4 to 7 days). Dormant D-HEp3 and BM-HEp3 cells were inoculated into CAMs at 500 × 10<sup>3</sup> cells per egg. For atRA <i>in vivo</i> experiments, 150 × 10<sup>3</sup> T-HEp3 cells were resuspended in 50 μl PBS in the presence of atRA (2 μM) or DMSO and inoculated into each CAM. Twenty-four hours post inoculation 50 μl of PBS plus atRA (2 μM) or DMSO were added on top of each egg. On day 4, the same treatment was repeated. Tumours were harvested on day 5, minced, digested with collagenase-1A (Sigma—C9891) for 30 min at 37 °C and tumour cells, recognized by their very large diameter, were counted with a hemocytometer. For serial transplantation studies of HEp3 tumours on CAM, HEp3 tumours were minced and dissociated with collagenase-IA, tumour cells were counted and a single-cell suspension (150 × 10<sup>3</sup> cells in 50 μl PBS) from each tumour was reinoculated on CAMs. For RNA interference studies, transfections of cells with siRNAs targeting the desired sequences or control scrambled siRNA were performed as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Adam, A. P. et al. Computational identification of a p38SAPK-regulated transcription factor network required for tumor cell quiescence. Cancer Res. 69, 5664–5672 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2528">3</a></sup>. Twenty-four hours after transfection 150 × 10<sup>3</sup> cells were inoculated on the CAM of 9–10-day-old chicken embryos (Charles River). Four days post inoculation, the number of tumour cells per nodule was counted. siRNAs knockdown were analysed with QPCR.</p><p>For the HEp3 xenograft studies, 750 × 10<sup>3</sup> cells were injected subcutaneously in 8-week-old female BALB/c nu/nu mice (Jackson Laboratories) in the interscapular region. Mice were inspected every 48 h and arising tumours were measured with calipers in two perpendicular diameters. Mice were kept (~14 days) until primary tumours developed and grew up to ~500–800 mm<sup>3</sup>. Mice were then injected with anaesthetics ketamine 80–120 mg i.p. and xylazine 5 mg i.p, and an incision of ~1 cm above the base of the tumour was performed. This allows gently scoop-out the tumour tissue and leaving sufficient skin to close the wound with sutures. Sutures were performed using a wound clipper. In the DOX-induced depletion of NR2F1 studies, mice were injected i.p. with 25 mg kg<sup>−g</sup> of DOX or vehicle every 48 h. For the MMTV-myc tumour studies, 1,000 or 10,000 MMTV-Myc-vector or MMTV-Myc-NR2F1 cells were orthotopically injected in the mammary fat pad of synergetic FvB mice. Tumours were detectable from day 7 and were measured every other day. Animals were killed when tumours reached 500 mm<sup>3</sup> size.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">IHC and IF assays</h3><p>Paraffin-embedded sections from human HNSCC tumours and lymph node metastasis and breast carcinoma tissues were stained for NR2F1 expression after quenching endogenous peroxidase activity. Binding of the primary antibody was carried out at 4 °C overnight, detected by anti-rabbit secondary antibody (1 h at room temperature) and revealed using DAB Chromagen. Paraffin-embedded sections from T-HEp3 and D-HEp3 tumours were stained for the followed antigens: SOX9 (dilution of Ab 1:150 for D-HEp3 and 1:75 for T-HEp3), NR2F1 (1:100 for D-HEp3 and 1:50 for T-HEp3) and Ki67 (dilution of ab 1:100). For immunofluorescence assays, tumour cells seeded in coverslips were fixed with 4% paraformaldehyde (PFA) in PBS and were permeabilized with 0.1% Triton X-100. Cells were then blocked with PBS containing 3% normal goat serum.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Gene expression profiling from T- and D-HEp3 cells <i>in vivo</i></h3><p>GFP-tagged T-HEp3 and D-HEp3 cells were inoculated and allowed to grow (T-HEp3) or enter dormancy (D-HEp3) for 1 week. Then 5 T-HEp3 tumours or 5 D-HEp3 dormant nodules were processed for FACS and GFP+ cells were used to extract RNA. This RNA (10 μg) was used to perform gene expression profiling using the Affymetrix HG-U133-PLUS2 arrays. The analysis of the gene expression profiles was performed as described in ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Adam, A. P. et al. Computational identification of a p38SAPK-regulated transcription factor network required for tumor cell quiescence. Cancer Res. 69, 5664–5672 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2564">3</a> and the data are deposited in the Gene Expression Omnibus (GEO) under the accession number GSE58588. Additional methods can be found to be associated with the profiles in the GEO database. The following link has been created to allow review of record GSE58588 while it remains in private status: <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/query/acc.cgi?token=ovilmukmxluvpaj&amp;acc=GSE58588">http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/query/acc.cgi?token=ovilmukmxluvpaj&amp;acc=GSE58588</a></p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Human prostate cancer DTC isolation and expression profiling</h3><p>Individual prostate cancer DTCs were isolated from the bone marrow of post-radical prostatectomy patients with NED (dormant disease) or ADV<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Chery, L. et al. Characterization of single disseminated prostate cancer cells reveals tumor cell heterogeneity and identifies dormancy associated pathways. Oncotarget 5, 9939–9951 (2014)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2582">31</a></sup>. Samples were obtained from patients who signed written informed consent. The study was approved by the Cancer Consortium institutional review board at the Fred Hutchinson Cancer Research Center and the University of Washington (Protocol no. 7350). NED patients showed undetectable PSA level (&lt;0.1 ng ml<sup>−g</sup>) 7–18 years after prostatectomy. ADV patients showed disease progression with failed treatment or existing distant metastasis. Seven individual NED cells (four patients) and 37 ADV cells (six patients) were amplified using the WT-OviationTM one-direct system (NuGEN) and single-cell gene expression was analysed by Agilent 4 × 44 K Human Microarrays. The analysis of the gene expression profiles of single cells obtained from patients with advanced disease and patients with no evidence of disease was performed on the profiles in GEO database under the accession number GSE48995. All isolation methods and procedures are described therein. The following link has been created to allow review of record GSE48995 while it remains in private status: <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/query/acc.cgi?token=ndclzsckieyygte&amp;acc=GSE48995">http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/query/acc.cgi?token=ndclzsckieyygte&amp;acc=GSE48995</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Senescence assays</h3><p>Tumours were harvested, digested with collagenase-1A (Sigma—C9891) for 30 min at 37 °C and then the manufacturer’s protocol for senescence detection (Cell Signaling, no. 9860S) was followed.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">RNA interference</h3><p>Transfections of siRNAs targeting the desired sequences or scrambled siRNA were performed using siPORT NeoFX (Ambion). Detached cells were resuspended and overlaid on the transfection complexes. After 24 h under normal cell culture conditions, cells were either used for <i>in vivo</i> experiments or lysed for immunoblotting and/or qPCR. Human siNR2F1 sequence sense: 5′-CUCUCAUCCGCGAUAUGUU-3′; antisense: 5′-AACAUAUCGCGGAUGAGAG-3′ (SIGMA, SASI_Hs01_00095428/AS). Human siNANOG sequence sense: 5′-GUGUGUACUCAAUGAUAGA-3′, antisense: 5′-UCUAUCAUUGAGUACACAC-3′ (SIGMA, SASI_Hs01_00163355/AS). Human siSOX9 sequence sense: 5′-CGUGUGAUCAGUGUGCUAA-3′; antisense: 5′-UUAGCACACUGAUCACACG-3′ (SIGMA, SASI_Hs01_00240733/AS). Human siRARβ sequence sense: 5′-CAUUGCUGGAAGCACUAAA-3′; antisense: 5′-UUUAGUGCUUCCAGCAAUG-3′ (SIGMA, SASI_Hs01_00237846/AS).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Immunoblotting, RT–PCR and qPCR</h3><p>Images for immunoblotting have been cropped for presentation. Full-size images are presented in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Figs 7–11</a>. RT–PCR and qPCR were performed as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Adam, A. P. et al. Computational identification of a p38SAPK-regulated transcription factor network required for tumor cell quiescence. Cancer Res. 69, 5664–5672 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2625">3</a></sup>. NR2F1 human forward primer: 5′-GCCTCAAAGCCATCGTGCTG-3′, NR2F1 human reverse primer: 5′-CCTCACGTACTCCTCCAGTG-3′, SOX9 human forward primer: 5′-CACTCCTCCTCCGGCATG-3′, SOX9 human reverse primer: 5′-GGCTGCACGTCGGTTTTGG-3′, RARβ human forward primer: 5′-GTACCACTATGGGGTCAGCG-3′, RARβ human reverse primer: 5′-CGACAGTATTGGCATCGATTCC-3′, NANOG human forward primer: 5′-CTAAGAGGTGGCAGAAAAACA-3′, NANOG human reverse primer: 5′-CTGGTGGTAGGAAGAGTAAAGG-3′, SOX2 human forward primer: 5′-GTTGTCAAGGCAGAGAAGAG-3′, SOX2 human reverse primer: 5′-GAGAGAGGCAAACTGGAATC-3′, TUBULIN human forward primer: 5′-CCCTCCAAGCTCTACTCT-3′, TUBULIN human reverse primer: 5′-GACCAAGGCTGGTCTCTTTC-3′, HES-1 human forward primer: 5′-CCCAGCCAGTGTCAACACGACA-3′, HES-1 human reverse primer: 5′-GGTACCGCCCTTACCTTTCTGTGC-3′, p27 human forward primer: 5′-GGTTAGCGGAGCAATGCGCA-3′, p27 human reverse primer: 5′-AACCGGCATTTGGGGAACCGTC-3′, p15 human forward primer: 5′-TGGGGTGGGAAAGTGGATTGCA-3′, p15 human reverse primer: 5′-CCCAGTGCAGAGGTGTTCAGGTCT-3′, p16 human forward primer: 5′-GCTGCTCACCTCTGGTGCCAAA-3′ and p16 human reverse primer: 5′-ACCTGCGCACCATGTTCTCG-3′.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Detection of DTCs via <i>Alu</i>-qPCR</h3><p>Human cells within mouse BM, lungs and spleen were detected by real-time <i>Alu</i>-qPCR, essentially as described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Zijlstra, A. et al. A quantitative analysis of rate-limiting steps in the metastatic cascade using human-specific real-time polymerase chain reaction. Cancer Res. 62, 7083–7092 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2645">47</a></sup>. Briefly, BM flushes were snap-frozen in liquid nitrogen and the genomic DNA was extracted using the Extract-N-Amp kit from Sigma (XNAT2-1KT). Genomic DNA (30 ng) was used per PCR reaction. Amplification of mice <i>GAPDH</i> was used as an internal control for the total amount of tissue. The ratio between <i>hAlu</i> and <i>mGAPDH</i> is used for comparative purposes between experimental groups. The primers for <i>hAlu</i> sequences and mice <i>GAPDH</i> were: <i>hAlu</i> sense (5′-ACGCCTGTAATCCCAGCACTT-3′) and <i>hAlu</i> antisense (5′-TCGCCCAGGCTGGGTGCA-3′), <i>mGAPDH</i> FW (5′-ATGGTGAAGGTCGGTGTGA-3′) and <i>mGAPDH</i> RV (5′-CGTTGATGGCAACAATCTC-3′). Each assay included two negative controls (water and genomic DNA extracted from BM, lung and spleen from a mouse that has not been injected with human cells), a positive control (human genomic DNA) and the experimental samples in triplicate. The actual number of tumour cells present in each tissue sample was determined using a standard curve generated by serial dilution of HEp3 cells. Briefly, organ-specific cells were collected from nude mice that did not bear tumours. HEp3 cells were serially diluted into mice organ-specific single-cell suspensions and the genomic DNA was extracted and amplified. The actual number of tumour cells could be determined over a range of 10–1,000 cells per BM, 100–10,000 cells per spleen and over 100–1,000,000 cells per lung.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Sphere-forming assay</h3><p>After the pre-reprogramming protocol, T-HEp3 cells were seeded in low-attachment six-multi-well plates (100 × 10<sup>3</sup> cells per well) in stem cell media (DMEM:F12, B27 supplement, EGF). Sphere formation was observed by light microscope and spheres were counted on day 6. MMTV-myc cells: MMTV-myc control cells or MMTV-myc-Nr2f1 cells were trypsinized, dissociated by pipetting and filtering through a 40-μm pore cell strainer. In all, 1 × 10<sup>5</sup> cells per condition were plated by triplicates in ultralow adhesion six-well plates (Corning, Corning, NY, USA) and incubated in serum-free F12/DMEM 50:50 medium (Cellgro, Mediatech Inc, Manassas, VA, USA) supplemented with 20 ng ml<sup>−l</sup> EGF and 1:50 B27 Supplement (Invitrogen, Carlsbad, CA, USA).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Flow cytometry</h3><p>For the characterization of the progenitor containing compartments, each well was dissociated with PBS-EDTA at 37 °C with gentle shaking, filtered through a 40-μm cell strainer and incubated with PE or APC-conjugated anti-CD24 Ab (BD Pharmingen, San Jose, CA, USA), APC-conjugated anti-CD29 Ab (Invitrogen, Camarillo, CA, USA).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Sequenom array</h3><p>The bisulfite PCR primers for the Sequenom array were designed using the EpiTYPER software (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Table III</a>). Genomic DNA from each sample was prepared using the QIAamp DNA mini kit (Qiagen) and the concentration and quality were determined by measuring the absorbance at 260 and 280 nm in a spectrophotometer (NanoDrop 8000, Thermo Scientific). DNA was subjected to bisulfite modification by using the EZ DNA Methylation kit (Zymo Research). Purified DNA samples were forwarded to Sequenom MassARRAY core facility at the Cornell University Institute of Biothechnology.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">ChIP</h3><p>ChIP was performed as previously described and followed by qPCR analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Gaspar-Maia, A. et al. MacroH2A histone variants act as a barrier upon reprogramming towards pluripotency. Nat. Commun. 4, 1565 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms7170#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d34800014e2718">48</a></sup>. The antibodies used for the immunoprecipitation were the following: H3K4me3 (ab1012, Abcam), H3K9me3 (ab8898, Abcam) and H3K27ac (ab4729, Abcam). Real-time PCR reactions (Roche) were performed in triplicates using primers for the correspondent genomic regions. Fold enrichment over 1% input was calculated using the 2DeltaCt method. Primers for ChiP are described in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms7170#MOESM627">Supplementary Table IV</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Statistical analysis</h3><p>For DTC detection experiments, the <i>P</i> values were estimated using Mann–Whitney nonparametric test with one-tailed <i>P</i> values≤0.05 considered significant. For all the rest of the experiments unpaired <i>t</i>-test were used with a 95% confidence. For <i>in vitro</i> experiments, a minimum of triplicates per group and repetition of at least three times was applied to achieve reproducibility. For <i>in vivo</i> experiments a minimum of five tumours for CAM assays and a minimum of eight mice was assessed to achieve reproducibility.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Additional information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec28-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec28">Additional information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec28-content"><p><b>Accession codes</b>. Gene Expression Omnibus under the accession number <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/query/acc.cgi?acc=GSE58588">GSE58588</a> and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/query/acc.cgi?acc=GSE48995">GSE48995</a>.</p><p><b>How to cite this article:</b> Sosa, M. S. <i>et al.</i> NR2F1 controls tumour cell dormancy via SOX9- and RARβ-driven quiescence programmes. <i>Nat. Commun.</i> 6:6170 doi: 10.1038/ncomms7170 (2015).</p></div></div></section>
